وإنما تشملهم بمعنى الإبعاد عن الرحمة المختصة. والله أعلم.
A benefit: From what is attributed to Ibn al-Mubarak (may Allah have mercy on him) of poems in praise of Imam Abu Hanifa, as stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others: 'By the honor of the lands and those upon them / The Imam of the Muslims is Abu Hanifa / With rulings and traces and jurisprudence / Like the verses of the Psalms on the page / So there is no one in the east his equal / Nor in the west nor in Kufa / He became a light in Islam / Trustworthy to the Messenger and the Caliph / He spends the night active in prayer / And guards his tongue from every falsehood / And his limbs abstain from forbidden things and amusements / And the pleasure of Allah is his task / So who is like Abu Hanifa in his eminence / An Imam for all creation and people / I have seen his detractors as foolish / Opposing the truth with weak arguments.' Then the objection: 'And may the curse of our Lord be counted as sand / Upon those who reject the saying of Abu Hanifa.' The objection is raised that this contradicts hadiths prohibiting cursing any Muslim, and that cursing is only permissible against disbelievers, not believers. The answer: 'Cursing specific to disbelievers is in the sense of absolute removal from mercy, not absolute in the sense of excluding believers, rather it includes them in the sense of removal from special mercy. Then cursing...'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a poem attributed to 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak in praise of Abu Hanifa, celebrating his unparalleled status in fiqh, his piety, and his role as a beacon of Islam. The poem appears in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and other sources. The commentator notes a critical issue with the last line: it invokes a curse (la'n) upon those who reject Abu Hanifa's view. This raises a problem because hadiths prohibit cursing a Muslim. The objection is that the poem seems to permit cursing fellow Muslims. The response distinguishes two meanings of la'n: (1) in the absolute sense (mutlaq), meaning removal from Allah's mercy entirely—this is only for disbelievers; (2) in a restricted sense, meaning removal from the special mercy (al-rahmah al-mukhtassah) reserved for the righteous—this is permissible for Muslims who commit sins. Thus, the poem's curse is understood in the second sense, not as a call for damnation. The commentator also adds a skeptical footnote from al-Lakhnawi, questioning whether all these verses are genuinely from Ibn al-Mubarak due to chronological difficulties (Ibn al-Mubarak died before al-Shafi'i's time, yet the poem mentions al-Shafi'i indirectly).

Key Terms
لعن (la'n): A curse; invoking removal from Allah's mercy. The passage distinguishes between absolute (eternal) curse for disbelievers and restricted (temporal) curse for believers.
الدر المختار (al-Durr al-Mukhtar): A major Hanafi fiqh reference work by Muhammad al-Haskafi, a commentary on Tanwir al-Absar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, in what sense is cursing a Muslim permissible?
A) In the absolute sense of removal from all mercy
B) In the sense of removal from special mercy (al-rahmah al-mukhtassah)
C) In the sense of praying for their death
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on la'n hinges on whether the curse is absolute (eternal damnation, only for disbelievers) or restricted (removal from special mercy, permissible for sinful Muslims).

8 min
When Cursing and Rebuttal Are Permissible vs. Forbidden
Clarifies when cursing and rebuttal are permissible or impermissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cursing specific vs. unspecified persons, explain when rebuttal of a scholar becomes forbidden, and identify the consequence of contemptuous rebuttal on legal testimony.

Core Idea
Cursing a specific person is forbidden, but cursing unspecified sinners is permissible; contemptuous rebuttal of a mujtahid imam is a major sin.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a crucial ethical issue in Islamic scholarship: how to distinguish between permissible scholarly disagreement and forbidden contemptuous rejection. The author uses prophetic hadiths to establish the general rule on cursing, then applies it to the specific case of rebutting Imam Abu Hanifa. The key is the attitude and manner of rebuttal: respectful disagreement is a long-standing tradition among scholars, but degrading insult is a major sin that disqualifies one from giving testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings on cursing
The passage opens by stating that cursing a specific person is not allowed, but cursing unspecified categories of sinners is permissible. This is the foundational distinction.
2
Examine the prophetic evidence
The author cites hadiths where the Prophet (SAW) cursed general groups (hair-joiners, tattooers, etc.) to show that general cursing is permitted when based on Shariah texts.
3
Understand the objection
The author anticipates a question: if rebutting Abu Hanifa seems forbidden, how is it that scholars have always disagreed and critiqued each other?
4
Apply the distinction
The author clarifies that the forbidden rebuttal is not mere disagreement, but contemptuous rejection that degrades the Imam, insults him, and harms his followers. Respectful scholarly debate is excluded.
5
Note the legal consequence
Such contemptuous behavior makes one a fasiq (transgressor) whose testimony is rejected. Insulting the Salaf (including mujtahid imams) is specifically prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المسلمين لا يجوز على شخص معيَّن، وأما على غير المعين فجائز، كما ورد في الأخبار : من لعن الواصلة والمستوصلة، والواشمة والمستوشمة، والمتشبهات بالرجال، والمتشبهين بالنساء، ولعن من غير منار الأرض، ومن ذبح لغير الله، إلى غير ذلك من العصاة. فإن قلت : كيف يكون مجرَّدُ الردّ على أبي حنيفة لله باعثاً للعن والإبعاد، ولم يزل العلماء والمجتهدون يردّ بعضهم بعضاً، ويطعن بعضهم بعضاً في استدلال بعض؟ قلت : ليس المراد بالردّ مطلق الردّ، بل ردّ ما قاله من الأحكام الشرعية محتقراً لها، أو ردّ طرقه واستدلاله إلى حد يحطّه عن منزله، ويحقره ويؤذي مقلديه، ويصل إلى حد سبّه وشتمه وإطلاق كلمات قبيحة عليه على ما هو الشائع في أكثر العوام، بل الخواص كالعوام. فإنّ مثل هذا الردّ على مثل هذا الإمام الذي أقرّ بفضله المجتهدون، وشهد بعلمه وفقهه وتقواه وورعه واجتهاده وانقياده للشريعة واتباعه للطريقة الأئمة المرضيون يبلغ فاعله إلى أن يصير ملعونا مردود الشهادة فاسقاً مطروداً، معدوداً في أهل الضلالة وقد منع الفقهاء من قبول شهادة من يظهرُ سب السلف، وفسره شارح الوقاية وصاحب النهاية وغيرهما بالصحابة والتابعين والأئمة المجتهدين، فاحفظه ولا تكن من الغافلين.
It is not permissible for Muslims to curse a specific person, but it is permissible regarding an unspecified one, as reported in the traditions: 'May Allah curse the hair-joiner and the one who asks for joining,' and 'the tattooer and the one who gets tattooed,' and 'women who imitate men and men who imitate women,' and 'cursing the one who changes the landmarks of the land, and the one who slaughters for other than Allah,' and other such curses upon sinners. If you say: How can mere rebuttal of Abu Hanifa for Allah's sake be a cause for curse and expulsion, while scholars and mujtahids have always rebutted each other and criticized each other's reasoning? I say: What is meant by rebuttal is not absolute rebuttal, but rebuttal of what he said of legal rulings with contempt for them, or rebuttal of his methods and reasoning to the extent of degrading him from his rank, belittling him, harming his followers, and reaching the level of insulting and abusing him and uttering foul words against him, as is common among most commoners, nay even the elite are like commoners. Indeed, such rebuttal of such an Imam, whose virtue the mujtahids have affirmed, and whose knowledge, jurisprudence, piety, god-fearing, ijtihad, submission to the Sharia, and following of the path the approved imams have testified, leads the doer to become cursed, rejected in testimony, a transgressor, expelled, counted among the people of misguidance. The jurists have prohibited accepting the testimony of one who manifests insult of the Salaf, and the commentator of al-Wiqayah and the author of al-Nihayah and others have interpreted it as the Companions, the Followers, and the mujtahid imams. So remember this and do not be among the heedless.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion on the ruling of cursing (la'n) and rebuttal of scholars, particularly Imam Abu Hanifa. The author begins by stating the general rule: cursing a specific individual is not permissible, but cursing unspecified sinners is allowed, as evidenced by several hadiths that curse categories of people (e.g., those who change landmarks). He then addresses a potential objection: how can rebutting Abu Hanifa be a cause for curse when scholars regularly disagree? The answer distinguishes between respectful scholarly debate and contemptuous rejection that degrades the Imam, insults him, and harms his followers. Such contemptuous behavior renders the person a sinner whose testimony is rejected. The passage also clarifies that 'insulting the Salaf' includes the Companions, Followers, and mujtahid imams like Abu Hanifa. This is a ruling on social and religious ethics within the Hanafi school, emphasizing respect for scholars.

Key Terms
الردّ (ar-radd): Rebuttal or refutation of someone's opinion.
العن (al-la'n): Cursing; invocation of Allah's distance and displeasure.
سب السلف (sabb as-salaf): Insulting the early generations, including Companions, Followers, and mujtahid imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when does rebuttal of Imam Abu Hanifa become forbidden?
A) Any rebuttal of his rulings is forbidden.
B) Only when done in a respectful scholarly manner.
C) When it involves contempt, degrading him, insulting him, or harming his followers.
D) When done by a non-mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
Contemptuous rebuttal that degrades a mujtahid imam is a major sin that disqualifies one from testifying; only respectful scholarly disagreement is permissible.

Biographical Entries in Fiqh Commentaries: Barira, al-Bazdawi, and al-Tirmidhi
Biographical sketches with fiqh significance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal ruling derived from Barira's story (khiyar al-‘itq) and identify the authority of al-Bazdawi and al-Tirmidhi in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Biographies in fiqh texts anchor legal rulings to real people and authorities.

This lesson introduces four figures mentioned in the commentary. While Burhan al-Islam is noted for a poetic aside, Barira's story provides a key legal precedent, and the biographies of al-Bazdawi and al-Tirmidhi establish their scholarly credentials. Understanding who these figures are helps us assess the authority of the rulings they are associated with.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figures and their fiqh connection
Read each entry and note why the commentator included it. Burhan al-Islam is quoted in a discussion on trade; Barira's case is a source of a legal ruling; al-Bazdawi is a Hanafi authority; al-Tirmidhi is a hadith scholar whose work is referenced.
The entries are not random; they support arguments and evidence in the commentary.
2
Extract the legal ruling from Barira's story
Focus on the detail that the Prophet gave Barira the choice to annul her marriage upon emancipation. This is the foundation of the Hanafi rule of khiyar al-‘itq – the option of a freed slave woman to dissolve her marriage.
The passage states she chose separation, confirming the option exists.
3
Note the authoritative works of al-Bazdawi
Al-Bazdawi authored key Hanafi texts like al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. Recognizing his works helps you understand the chain of transmission within the school.
He is a major Hanafi faqih of the 5th century AH.
4
Appreciate al-Tirmidhi's role as a hadith source
Al-Tirmidhi is cited in the discussion on the sunan of wudu. His Sunan is one of the six canonical collections, but Hanafis may critically evaluate its hadith based on matn (content).
His inclusion shows the integration of hadith evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

The Ninth Study: Biographies of Notables in the Commentary
18. Burhan al-Islam: He is mentioned in the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') in the discussion on the dislike of intercepting caravans for trade (talab al-jalb). He said: 'I heard some fine verses by our master Burhan al-Islam, so I wrote them down: Abi Bakr, the chosen son, wanted to go out for a wondrous matter. Then he said: I have resolved to go out for an expiation.' End.
19. Barira (may Allah be pleased with her): She is mentioned in the Book of Disliked Actions (Kitab al-Karahiyya) in the discussion on accepting gifts. She was a freed slave (mawla) of Aisha al-Siddiqa (may Allah be pleased with her). She was owned by some of Banu Hilal, or some say by some Ansar, or by Abu Ahmad ibn Jahsh. They made a kitaba (contract of emancipation) with her, then sold her to Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), who bought and freed her. Her husband's name was Mughith. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) gave her the choice upon emancipation, and she chose separation from him. The stories of Aisha's purchase of her, freeing her, giving her the choice to leave her husband, and her gift to the Prophet (peace be upon him) are narrated in the six authentic collections and others.
20. al-Bazdawi (may Allah have mercy on him): He is mentioned in the Chapter on Maintenance (al-Nafaqa) from the Book of Marriage (al-Nikah). He is Abu al-Usr, Fakhr al-Islam, Ali ibn Muhammad al-Bazdawi, attributed to Bazda (with fatha), a place name. He was among the great Hanafi scholars. He authored 'al-Mabsut', 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir', 'al-Jami' al-Kabir', 'Tafsir al-Quran', and 'Sharh Sahih al-Bukhari', and others. He died in the year 482 H. This is from 'Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' and others.
21. al-Tirmidhi (may Allah have mercy on him): He is mentioned in the discussion of the sunan of wudu. He is Abu Isa Muhammad ibn Isa ibn Sura (with fatha) al-Tirmidhi, attributed to Tirmidh, a famous town on the banks of the Balkh river. The name is pronounced with kasra of the ta' and mim (most famous), or with damma, or with fatha of the first and kasra of the third.
(1) See Sahih al-Bukhari (1:173) and Sahih Muslim (4:249), etc. (2) In the original it says 'four' but the correct from biographical works is 'eighty-two'. (3) Not 848 as some of our contemporaries wrote in the treatise 'al-Hitta bi Dhikr al-Sihah al-Sitta' (p. 194) and in 'al-It'haf' when mentioning the commentators of Sahih al-Bukhari. (4) Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' (18:602-603). May Allah have mercy on him. (5) See al-Jawahir (2:594-595), Taj al-Tarajim (p. 205), Muqaddimat al-Hidaya (3:14), al-Fawa'id (pp. 209-211), and A'lam al-Akhyar (folios 156b-157b).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is the ninth study (الدراسة التاسعة) of the commentary, dedicated to biographies of notable individuals mentioned in the sharh. It provides brief accounts of four figures:

18. Burhan al-Islam: He is mentioned in the book of sales, specifically on the issue of intercepting caravans (talab al-jalb), which is disliked. The author includes a poetic piece by him.

19. Barira (رضي الله عنها): A well-known companion and freed slave of Aisha. Her story involves being freed, then given the choice to stay with her husband Mughith or leave; she chose separation. This serves as a legal precedent for the option of emancipation (khiyar al-‘itq) in Hanafi fiqh. The author cites that her story is in all six major hadith collections.

20. al-Bazdawi: A major Hanafi scholar, Abu al-Usr Fakhr al-Islam Ali ibn Muhammad al-Bazdawi. He is the author of several important Hanafi texts including al-Mabsut, Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and a commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari. He died in 482 AH. The passage corrects a mistaken date of 848.

21. al-Tirmidhi: The famous hadith scholar, Abu Isa Muhammad ibn Isa al-Tirmidhi, author of Sunan al-Tirmidhi. He is mentioned in the discussion of the sunan of wudu. The passage notes the correct pronunciation of his nisba.

The footnotes provide references to hadith collections and biographical works.

Key Terms
بريرة (Barira): A female companion (sahabiyya), formerly a slave of Aisha, whose story of manumission and choice regarding her husband is a legal precedent.
البَزْدَوِي (al-Bazdawi): A Hanafi jurist (faqih) from the 5th century AH, known for his works in Hanafi fiqh and usul.
التَّرْمِذِي (al-Tirmidhi): A famous hadith scholar, author of Sunan al-Tirmidhi, one of the six canonical hadith collections.
خيار العتق (khiyar al-‘itq): The option granted to a slave woman upon emancipation to annul her marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What legal ruling (hukm) is derived from the story of Barira (رضي الله عنها)?
A) That a freed slave woman must remain with her husband.
B) That a freed slave woman has the option (khiyar) to annul her marriage.
C) That a slave woman cannot be freed while married.
D) That the husband of a freed slave woman must also be freed.

Key Takeaway
Biographical entries in Hanafi commentaries often contain key legal precedents, such as Barira's khiyar al-‘itq, and establish the authority of scholars like al-Bazdawi and al-Tirmidhi.

Identifying the Prophet's Uncles and Key Hanafi Transmitters
Biographical list of the Prophet's uncles and later Hanafi scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the twelve paternal uncles of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and explain the significance of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Khwaharzadah in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Knowing the Prophet's family and later scholars is essential for tracing riwayah and understanding references in fiqh texts.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya introduces us to the paternal uncles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and two important Hanafi figures. While not a legal ruling, this biographical knowledge is crucial for any student of Hanafi fiqh, as these individuals appear in chains of transmission and are referenced in later works. Understanding their identities helps us contextualize the sources and appreciate the scholarly heritage of the school.

Guided Path
1
List the Twelve Uncles
Read through the list and note each uncle's name, his relationship to the Prophet, and whether he embraced Islam. Pay attention to details like kunya and nicknames.
The text counts 'Abdullah, the Prophet's father, as the thirteenth son of 'Abd al-Muttalib, but he is not an uncle.
2
Classify Each Uncle by Islamic Status
For each uncle, determine if he died before Islam, became a Muslim, or remained a non-Muslim. Note which of their children became Companions.
This classification is important for understanding hadith narration chains.
3
Identify the Hanafi Figures
Read the entries for al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Khwaharzadah. Note their full names, teachers, works, and significance in the Hanafi school.
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad is a major transmitter of the Nawadir; Khwaharzadah wrote influential texts.
4
Connect to Fiqh References
Consider why a fiqh commentary would include these biographies. How does knowing these figures help when you encounter their names in later chapters?
This biographical knowledge provides context for understanding the sources of rulings.

The ninth study: Biographies of eminent persons in the commentary. (2) 177 And al-Qastalani in al-Mawahib al-Laduniyya, and al-Zurqani (1) in his commentary: That the Messenger of Allah had twelve paternal uncles, and his father Abdullah was their thirteenth: The first: al-Harith ibn Abd al-Muttalib, who was his eldest child, and he witnessed with him the digging of Zamzam, and died during his father's lifetime, and did not live to see Islam. His sons Abu Sufyan, Nawfal, Rabi'a, al-Mughira, and Abdullah were all Companions. The second: Abu Talib, neither he nor his son Talib embraced Islam, but his three sons Aqil, Ja'far, and Ali al-Murtada embraced Islam, and their sister Umm Hani (may Allah be pleased with her) also embraced Islam. The third: al-Zubayr, with diminutive form according to the majority, and said with fath of zay and kasr of ba, and his kunya is Abu al-Harith. He was a noble poet, chief of Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, did not live to see Islam, but his daughters Duba'a, Safiyya, Umm al-Hakam, and Umm al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with them) embraced Islam, and his son Abdullah embraced Islam. The fourth: Abu Lahab Abd al-Uzza, about whom Surah Tabbat was revealed; his two sons Utba and Muattib were from the Companions. The fifth: al-Ghaydaq, with fath of ghayn and dal, between them a sukun ya, his name was Mus'ab. The sixth: al-Muqawwam, in the passive form from al-taqwim, and he was the full brother of Hamza, and he was the seventh among them, he embraced Islam and was martyred in Uhud. The eighth: Dirar, with kasr, did not live to see Islam, died at its beginning, and he was the full brother of al-Abbas, and he was the ninth among them, he embraced Islam and became one of the great Companions. He died in the year 694. Thus in Mir'at al-Jinan (4:224). From him, may Allah have mercy on him. He is al-Hajjaj ibn Yusuf ibn Aqil al-Thaqafi, the oppressive governor who is proverbial for injustice. He was courageous, bold, awe-inspiring, eloquent, fluent, bloodthirsty, working for Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan, one of the Umayyad caliphs. He was governor of Hijaz for two years, then of Iraq and Khurasan for twenty years. He fought Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr and killed him, and violated the sanctity of the Meccan Haram. He continued corrupting and destroying until he died in Wasit, a town he built between Kufa and Basra, in the year 95, and Allah gave the lands and people relief from him. Thus in Tarikh al-Yafi'i. 26. al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: he is mentioned in the chapters of Hayd and Tayammum and others. He is al-Hasan al-Lu'lu'i al-Kufi, one of the esteemed students of Imam Abu Hanifa. He loved the Sunnah and memorized hadiths. He served as a judge then resigned. He wrote the books al-Mujarrad and al-Amali. He died in the year 204, and he was counted among those who renewed the religion of this Ummah at the turn of the second century. Thus in al-Athmar al-Janiyya. 27. Khwaharzadah: he is mentioned in the Book of Reviving Dead Lands. It is a title for many scholars who were sons of the sisters of famous virtuous persons. Commonly, in the books of our companions, two are known: One of them: Muhammad ibn al-Husayn al-Bukhari known as Bakr Khwaharzadah, from the greats of Transoxiana. He wrote al-Mukhtasar, al-Tajnish, and al-Mabsut known as Mabsut Khwaharzadah. He died in Jumada al-Ula in the year 383, or 373, or 333. (1) Mir'at al-Jinan (1:192-196). (2) In the books of biographies it is: al-Jarad. (3) al-Athmar al-Janiyya fi Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya (folio 17/a), and see: al-Jawahir (2:56-57), al-'Ibar (1:345), Tabaqat Ibn al-Hanai pp.18-19, and others.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a biographical section from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya, listing the paternal uncles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and later introducing other notable figures such as al-Hajjaj ibn Yusuf, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and Khwaharzadah. The author begins by quoting al-Qastalani and al-Zurqani that the Prophet had twelve uncles, with his father 'Abdullah being the thirteenth. Each uncle is described with details of their lineage, character, religious affiliation, and the status of their children as Companions. The text then moves to biographical entries for al-Hajjaj, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (a prominent Hanafi scholar and student of Abu Hanifa), and Khwaharzadah (a title for scholars who were nephews of famous figures). These biographies serve to identify individuals who appear elsewhere in the fiqh commentary, providing essential context for students of Hanafi fiqh. The passage includes editorial notes and references to other biographical works, reflecting the scholarly apparatus typical of later commentaries.

Key Terms
تراجم الأعيان (tarājim al-aʿyān): Biographies of eminent persons; a section of the commentary that provides biographical information about notable individuals mentioned in the main text.
أعمام النبي (aʿmām al-nabī): The paternal uncles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), enumerated here as twelve, with their father 'Abdullah counted separately.
الحسن بن زياد (al-Ḥasan ibn Ziyād): Al-Hasan al-Luʾluʾī al-Kūfī, a major student of Imam Abu Hanifa, known for his works al-Mujarrad and al-Amali, and considered a renewer of religion at the turn of the second century.
خواهر زاده (khwāhar zādah): A title meaning 'sister's son' (nephew), given to certain Hanafi scholars whose maternal uncles were famous. Commonly refers to Muhammad ibn al-Husayn al-Bukhari (Bakr Khwaharzadah) and another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Prophet had twelve paternal uncles, and knowing their biographies along with later scholars like al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Khwaharzadah is essential for understanding references in Hanafi fiqh works.

Khadijah bint Khuwaylid: The First Believer
Biographical entry for Umm al-Mu'minin Khadijah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the key biographical details of Khadijah bint Khuwaylid and explain the fiqh implication of the absence of funeral prayer at her death.

Core Idea
Khadijah was the first believer and mother of the Prophet's children; her burial without funeral prayer shows that some obligations were established later.

This lesson delves into the biography of Umm al-Mu'minin Khadijah, the first wife of the Prophet Muhammad and the first person to accept Islam. The passage not only provides her lineage and family history but also includes a subtle fiqh point: the funeral prayer was not performed for her because it had not yet been prescribed. Understanding this helps us appreciate the gradual revelation of Islamic laws.

Guided Path
1
Extract key biographical facts
List the essential details: her name, lineage, marital history, children, and date of death. Note the different opinions on her age at marriage and the year of death.
Focus on the relied-upon opinion: death three years before Hijrah.
2
Identify the fiqh reference
The author mentions she appears in the 'Book of Hudud' in the chapter on intercourse necessitating the hadd penalty. Consider why this reference is included in a biographical entry.
This cross-reference helps locate her mention in fiqh texts.
3
Analyze the funeral prayer note
The text states that no funeral prayer was performed because 'the funeral prayer had not been prescribed until that time.' Understand that the obligation of Salat al-Janazah was revealed later.
This illustrates the principle of tadarruj (gradual legislation) in Islam.
4
Connect to Hanafi fiqh
In the Hanafi school, the funeral prayer is a fard kifayah. The fact that it was not prescribed at Khadijah's death shows that some rulings were not present in the early Meccan period.
This helps in understanding the chronology of legal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خديجة رضي الله عنها : لها ذكر في باب وطء يوجب الحد» من «كتاب الحدود»، وهي أم المؤمنين خديجة - على وزن كبيرة – بنت خويلد بن أسد بن عبد العزى القرشية الأسدية ، أول من آمن بالنبي الله لم يتقدمها رجل ولا امرأة. وأول من تزوج بها رسول الله ، وهي أم أولاده الذكور والإناث، سوى إبراهيم ابن النبي ، فإنّه من مارية القبطية، وكانت قبل تزوجه تحت أبي هالة، ثمّ خلف عليها عتيق بن عائذ، ثمّ خلف عليها رسول الله ، تزوج بها وكان عمره خمساً وعشرين سنة ، وقيل : إحدى وعشرين سنة، وكان عمرها أربعين سنة. ولها مناقب وافرة مبسوطة في «الاستيعاب» و «أسد الغابة» وغيرهما من كتب أخبار الصحابة له ، وكانت وفاتُها قبل الهجرة بخمس سنين، وقيل: بأربع، وقيل: بثلاث، وهذا هوا المعتمد، في شهر رمضان بمكة، ودفنت بالحجون من غير أن يصلى عليها ؛ لأنَّ صلاة الجنازة لم تكن فرضت إلى ذلك الحين.
Khadijah, may Allah be pleased with her: She is mentioned in the chapter on intercourse that necessitates the hadd penalty from the Book of Hudud. She is the Mother of the Believers Khadijah - on the pattern of 'kabirah' - daughter of Khuwaylid ibn Asad ibn 'Abd al-'Uzza al-Qurashiyyah al-Asadiyyah. She was the first to believe in the Prophet of Allah, preceded by no man or woman. She was the first whom the Messenger of Allah married. She is the mother of his sons and daughters, except for Ibrahim the son of the Prophet, for he was from Mariyah al-Qibtiyyah. Before marrying him, she was under (married to) Abu Halah, then 'Utiq ibn 'A'idh married her, then the Messenger of Allah married her. He married her when he was twenty-five years old, and it is said twenty-one, and she was forty years old. She had abundant merits detailed in al-Isti'ab, Usd al-Ghabah, and other books of the biographies of the Companions. Her death occurred before the Hijrah by five years, and it is said by four, and it is said by three, and this is the relied-upon opinion, in the month of Ramadan in Mecca. She was buried in al-Hajun without a funeral prayer being performed over her because the funeral prayer had not been prescribed until that time.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Khadijah bint Khuwaylid, the first wife of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and the first person to accept Islam. The author notes that she is referenced in the Book of Hudud (fixed punishments) specifically in the chapter on intercourse that necessitates the hadd penalty. The biography provides her lineage, her marital history, her children (all except Ibrahim born from Mariyah), and her age at marriage. The author also clarifies that the funeral prayer was not performed for her because it had not been prescribed at that time. The biographical details are sourced from standard works like al-Isti'ab and Usd al-Ghabah. This entry helps the reader understand the context when Khadijah is mentioned in legal discussions.

Key Terms
خديجة (khadījah): Umm al-Mu'minin Khadijah bint Khuwaylid, first wife of the Prophet and first believer.
أم المؤمنين (umm al-mu'minīn): Title given to the wives of the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why was the funeral prayer not performed for Khadijah?
A) She was not a Muslim
B) The funeral prayer had not been prescribed yet
C) She died in a state of impurity
D) She was buried at night

Key Takeaway
Khadijah's burial without funeral prayer illustrates that certain Islamic obligations were revealed gradually and were not all in place during the early Meccan period.

Ahmad ibn 'Umar al-Khassaf: A Prolific Hanafi Jurist
Biographical entry on a Hanafi jurist

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key works and scholarly lineage of Ahmad ibn 'Umar al-Khassaf, a major Hanafi jurist.

Core Idea
Al-Khassaf authored foundational Hanafi works on waqf, hiyal, and adab al-qadi, and was known for his self-reliance.

This biographical passage introduces one of the most important Hanafi scholars of the third century: Ahmad ibn 'Umar al-Khassaf. Although mentioned here in passing, his works — especially on endowments (waqf), legal stratagems (hiyal), and judge's etiquette — have profoundly shaped Hanafi jurisprudence. Understanding his background helps us appreciate the depth and diversity of the school's literary tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure's full lineage and nisbah
Note the difference in his father's name ('Umar vs. 'Amr) as reported by different sources. This clarifies textual variants.
Al-Kafawi says 'Umar with damm, while al-Dhahabi says 'Amr with fatha.
2
Recognize his expertise and nickname
He was a faradi (inheritance scholar) and accountant. His nickname 'al-Khassaf' comes from his habit of eating from his own manual labor.
This shows his humility and independence.
3
Map his teachers and students
He studied under his father, Hasan ibn Ziyad, Abu Dawud al-Tayalisi, Musaddad, and 'Ali ibn al-Madini. This chain connects him to earlier Hanafi authorities.
Hasan ibn Ziyad was a direct student of Abu Hanifah.
4
Inventory his authored works
List the nine named books. Focus on those most referenced in Hanafi fiqh: Kitab Ahkam al-Waqf, Kitab al-Hiyal, Kitab Adab al-Qadi.
These works are still cited in later fatwa collections.
5
Understand his significance in the Hanafi school
Al-Khassaf's writings filled key lacunae in Hanafi jurisprudence, particularly on waqf and legal documentation.
His Kitab Ahkam al-Waqf is a foundational text on endowments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخصاف : له ذكر في باب النسب والحضانة» من «كتاب النكاح»، وفي «كتاب الشهادات»، هو أحمد بن عُمر بالضمّ على ما قاله الكفوي، أو عمرو بالفتح كما في «سير النبلاء للذهبي، كان فرضيّاً محاسباً عارفاً بمذهب أبي حنيفة ، لقب بالخصاف ؛ لأنه كان يأكل من صنعته بيده. روي عن أبيه، وهو تلميذ لحسن بن زياد، وعن أبي داود الطيالسي، ومُسَدَّد، وعلي بن المديني وغيرهم . وألف كتاب «مناسك الحج»، و«كتاب الحيل»، و«كتاب الوصايا»، و«كتاب الشروط»، و«كتاب المحاضر والسجلات»، و«كتاب الرضاع»، و«كتاب أدب القاضي»، و«كتاب النفقات على الأقارب»، و«كتاب أحكام الوقف»، وغير ذلك، مات سنة إحدى وستين ومئتين. كذا ذكره علي القاري والكفوي وغيرهما.
Al-Khassaf: He is mentioned in the chapter on lineage and custody from the Book of Marriage, and in the Book of Testimonies. He is Ahmad ibn 'Umar (with damm on 'Umar as al-Kafawi said, or 'Amr with fatha as in Siyar al-Nubala' of al-Dhahabi). He was a faradi (inheritance scholar) and accountant, knowledgeable in the school of Abu Hanifah. He was nicknamed al-Khassaf because he used to eat from his own craft (manual work). He narrated from his father, and he was a student of Hasan ibn Ziyad, and from Abu Dawud al-Tayalisi, Musaddad, 'Ali ibn al-Madini, and others. He authored the books: Manasik al-Hajj, Kitab al-Hiyal, Kitab al-Wasaya, Kitab al-Shurut, Kitab al-Mahadir wa al-Sijillat, Kitab al-Rada', Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Kitab al-Nafaqat 'ala al-Aqarib, Kitab Ahkam al-Waqf, and others. He died in the year 261 AH. Thus mentioned by 'Ali al-Qari, al-Kafawi, and others.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Ahmad ibn 'Umar al-Khassaf, a prominent Hanafi jurist and scholar. He is mentioned in the commentary in the context of the Book of Marriage (chapter on lineage and custody) and the Book of Testimonies. The author clarifies the correct pronunciation of his father's name (Umar vs 'Amr). Al-Khassaf was known for his expertise in inheritance law (faradi) and accounting, and he was deeply knowledgeable in the Hanafi madhhab. His nickname 'al-Khassaf' came from his practice of eating from his own manual labor. He studied under notable scholars including Hasan ibn Ziyad, a direct student of Abu Hanifah. The list of his authored works shows his breadth, covering hajj, legal stratagems (hiyal), wills, conditions, court records, fosterage, judge's etiquette, maintenance for relatives, and endowments. He died in 261 AH. This biography helps situate a key reference in the fiqh text.

Key Terms
الخصاف (al-khaṣṣāf): Nickname for Ahmad ibn 'Umar, a Hanafi jurist known for his works on fiqh.
فرضي (faraḍī): A scholar specialized in the law of inheritance (fara'id).

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Fiqh Significance of Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil)
Biographical entry linking Ibrahim to fiqh Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the significance of Prophet Ibrahim in Hanafi fiqh, identify his mention in the Book of Hajj, and explain why he is called al-Khalil and counted among ulu al-azm.

Core Idea
Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil) is foundational to Hajj rulings and holds the highest prophetic rank after Muhammad.

This passage introduces Prophet Ibrahim (Abraham) as he appears in the Hanafi fiqh text. Though brief, it establishes his unique status as 'al-Khalil' (the Friend of Allah) and places him among the five great prophets (ulu al-azm). More importantly, it flags that his actions and rites are central to the Book of Hajj, providing the basis for many legal rulings. Understanding Ibrahim's biography thus illuminates why his example is a primary source for Hajj practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject
The passage opens with 'al-Khalil' – identify that this title refers to Prophet Ibrahim (upon him be peace). Note his full name: Ibrahim ibn Azar.
Al-Khalil means 'the intimate friend' – Allah's special designation for Ibrahim.
2
Locate the Fiqh Reference
The text says 'he is mentioned in the Book of Hajj'. This tells you where in fiqh literature Ibrahim's rulings appear. Recall that Hajj rites like sa'i between Safa and Marwa, standing at Arafat, and stoning the Jamarat are all tied to Ibrahim's actions.
The Hanafi school derives many Hajj obligations from Ibrahim's practices (millat Ibrahim).
3
Understand Ibrahim's Prophetic Rank
The passage declares him 'the best of messengers after our master (Muhammad)' and 'one of ulu al-azm'. This means Ibrahim is second only to Prophet Muhammad in the hierarchy of prophets, and among the five with the highest resolve (Nuh, Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Muhammad).
Ulu al-azm prophets faced the greatest trials and are mentioned in Quran 46:35.
4
Note the Source for Further Details
Al-Tha'labi's work al-'Ara'is is cited as an elaboration of Ibrahim's stories and آثار (reports). This indicates that the fiqh text assumes knowledge of Ibrahim's biography from traditional tafsir and qiṣaṣ al-anbiyā' literature.
Other reliable sources include Surah Ibrahim, Surah al-Baqarah, and hadith.
5
Connect to a Specific Hajj Ruling
Example: The obligation of sa'i (walking between Safa and Marwa) is linked to Hajar's search for water, but the underlying foundation is Ibrahim's command to leave Hajar and Isma'il. The Hanafi ruling that sa'i is a wajib (necessary) not rukn is derived from this narrative.
Knowing Ibrahim's story helps distinguish between arkan (pillars) and wajibat in Hajj.

Source Text

أولي العزم (ulū al-‘azm): The five major prophets with firm resolve: Nuh, Ibrahim, Musa, 'Isa, and Muhammad.

Al-Khalil al-Lughawi: The Lexicographer Behind Hanafi Legal Interpretation
Biographical entry on a linguist cited in a Hanafi legal commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the significance of al-Khalil ibn Ahmad's linguistic works in the context of Hanafi fiqh and identify where his works are referenced in classical Hanafi texts.

Core Idea
Al-Khalil's Kitab al-'Ayn became the foundation for linguistic analysis in Hanafi legal reasoning.

This lesson introduces you to a key figure in Arabic linguistics whose work is often cited in Hanafi legal commentaries. Al-Khalil al-Lughawi's dictionary al-'Ayn and his systematic study of Arabic prosody ('arud) provided the tools for accurate interpretation of the Qur'an and Hadith, which are essential for istinbat (derivation of rulings). The text mentions that he is cited at the beginning of the Book of Ijarah, highlighting his relevance to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and his full lineage
Extract the full name and tribal affiliation from the Arabic text. This helps confirm his identity and authority.
His nisbah 'al-Farahidi' refers to a sub-tribe of Azd.
2
Recognize his major contributions
The text states he authored al-'Ayn and discovered 'arud. Understand why these are significant for Arabic linguistics.
'Arud is the science of poetic meter.
3
Locate his mention in Hanafi fiqh
The author notes that al-Khalil is mentioned at the beginning of the Book of Ijarah. Consider why a lexicographer would be cited in a legal work.
Ijarah (rental) contracts require precise language.
4
Analyze the chain of transmission
Note that he narrated from Ayyub and 'Asim al-Ahwal, and that Sibawayh studied under him. This shows his position in the scholarly network.
His teachers and students anchor his reliability.
5
Synthesize the relevance for fiqh
Conclude that accurate linguistic knowledge is a prerequisite for sound legal interpretation in the Hanafi school.
Usul al-fiqh relies on lexical meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخليل اللغوي : مؤلّف كتاب «العين»، له ذكر في أوائل «كتاب الإجارة»، الخليل بن أحمد بن عمرو بن تميم الأزدي الفراهيدي، نسبة إلى فراهيد، بطن من الأزد أبو عبد الرحمن البصري النحوي اللغوي، أول من استخرج العروض، وحصر أشعار العرب بها. هو روى عن أيوب وعاصم الأحول وغيرهما، وأخذ عنه سيبويه، وعامة الحكايات.
Al-Khalil al-Lughawi: Author of the book al-'Ayn. He is mentioned at the beginning of the Book of Ijarah. He is al-Khalil ibn Ahmad ibn 'Amr ibn Tamim al-Azdi al-Farahidi, relative to Farahid, a branch of Azd, Abu 'Abd al-Rahman al-Basri, the grammarian and lexicographer. He was the first to derive prosody ('arud) and to categorize the poetry of the Arabs by it. He narrated from Ayyub, 'Asim al-Ahwal, and others. Sibawayh studied under him, and most of the accounts (hikayat) are from him.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi, the renowned Arabic grammarian and lexicographer. He is mentioned in the commentary at the beginning of the Book of Ijarah (rentals) because his work on language is often cited. The author provides his full name and nisbah, noting that he was from the tribe of Azd, specifically the sub-tribe Farahid. Al-Khalil is credited with being the first to discover Arabic prosody ('arud) and to classify Arabic poetry using it. He was a teacher of the famous grammarian Sibawayh. This entry helps readers identify the source when al-Khalil al-Lughawi is referenced in legal discussions, particularly in cases involving linguistic interpretation.

Key Terms
الخليل اللغوي (al-khalīl al-lughawī): Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad, the classical Arabic lexicographer and grammarian, author of Kitab al-'Ayn.
العين (al-‘ayn): The first comprehensive Arabic dictionary, authored by al-Khalil ibn Ahmad.
العروض (al-‘arūḍ): The study of Arabic poetic meter, first systematized by al-Khalil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Martyrdom of Sheikh Nizam al-Din and the Hanafi Maxim of Specific Harm
Historical account of a Hanafi scholar's martyrdom and the application of a legal maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-darar al-khass yuhtamalu li-daf' al-darar al-'amm' and explain how it was applied in the crisis of Herat.

Core Idea
A specific harm is borne to avert a general harm—the ultimate sacrifice of a scholar in a Hanafi fatwa.

This lesson recounts the martyrdom of the Hanafi scholar Sheikh Nizam al-Din (known as Sheikh al-Taslim). Under dire circumstances, the people of Herat applied a central Hanafi legal maxim: that a specific harm (the loss of one life) may be borne to repel a general harm (the destruction of the entire city). We will examine the story, the maxim, and the legal reasoning behind this painful decision.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
Read the story of Sheikh Nizam al-Din's fatwa against the oppressive Turks and the subsequent siege of Herat. Identify the two harms at play: the specific harm of his death and the general harm of the city's destruction.
The Sultan was unable to defend the city.
2
Identify the legal maxim
The people of Herat issued a fatwa based on the principle that a specific harm (al-darar al-khass) can be borne to repel a general harm (al-darar al-'amm). State this maxim in Arabic and English.
This is a well-known Hanafi usul principle.
3
Apply the maxim to the scenario
Explain how the specific harm (handing over the mufti) was considered permissible to avoid the general harm (mass killing and destruction).
4
Reflect on the religious and ethical dimensions
Consider the sacrifice of the scholar and the role of necessity (darurah) in Islamic law. Why did the Sheikh willingly go?
He saw the fatwa as valid and chose to fulfill it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيخ يسمّى الإيمان الذي فسره العلماء بالتصديق والتسليم ، فلذلك سموه بشيخ التسليم، ومات شهيداً سنة ثمان وثلاثين وسبعمئة.
وسبب قتله : إنه كان جمع كثير من الأتراك في تلك النواحي مشغولين بالظلم والإضلال تاركين أحكام الشرع، فأفتى الشيخ نظام الدين بكفرهم، ولما وقفوا عليه جمعوا العسكر ووصلوا إلى هراة، ولم تكن للسلطان عند ذلك طاقة مقاومتهم ، ودفعهم فتحصن بحصن. فأرسلوا إليه سفيراً، وقالوا : غرضنا من اشتعال نار القتال قتل الذي أفتى بكفرنا لا غير، فإن كان المقصود حفظ أهل هراة وأموالهم وأولادهم فليخرجوا ذلك المفتي إلينا». ولما كان الأمرُ ضيّقاً بأهل هراة ووقعوا في الاضطرار والتحيّر، وقع الإفتاء منهم بأن تحمل الضرر الخاص لدفع الضرر العام جائز، وأرسلوه إلى الشيخ، فاطلع على مرادهم، فنزل عن المنبر وغسل ولبس أحسن الثياب، وخرج من البلد، فأخذه الظالمون عند ذلك وقتلوه. انتهى ملخصاً.
ومثله في روضات الجنات في فضائل هراة ، لكن ذكر فيه مقتله في ذي العقدة سبع وثلاثين وسبعمئة ، وقال ابنُ هبته فصيح الدين الهروي في شرحه للوقاية في كتاب الزكاة قال صدر الشريعة : فانظر إلى هذا الذي أدرج في الإيمان ركناً آخر، كيف يتمسك بهذه الرواية ، فسوّغ لولاة هراة أخذ العشور والزكاة بالصفة المعلومة، بل فرض عليهم ذلك ، وحكم بكفر من أنكره.
والصفة المعلومة أن يحرّض الأعونة في أخذ الخارج عن الأرض أضعافاً مضاعفة، فيضعوا على الملاك القيم، ويأخذوها جبراً وقهراً، ويصرفوها كما هو عادة أهل الإسراف والإتراف.
وأشار في هذا إلى جدّي من قبل الأم شيخ الإسلام الأعظم، إمام الأئمة الأعلام في العالم، محيي مراسم الدين بين الأمم ، الماحي سطوة سباع البدع وآثار الظلم، السعيد الشهيد نظام الملة والشريعة والتقوى والدين، عبد الرحيم الشهير بين أهل الإسلام بشيخ التسليم... الخ.
ثم أجاب عن إيرادات صدر الشريعة، ونصر جده، وحقق أقواله المنيفة، وستقف عليه في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى هذا آخر الكلام في هذا المقام. وقد ذكرت في مقدمة السعاية أحوال الكتب التي نقل عنها صدر الشريعة المسائل وغيرها، وأحوال النسب والقبائل والأمكنة المذكورة في شرح الوقاية، وتراجم شراح مختصر الوقاية.
ورأيت حذفها هاهنا أجدر طلباً للاقتصار على قدر الحاجة، وترك ما عنه غنية، وستطلعُ في الحاشية في كل موضع على ما يناسبها على وجه يفيد الطالب بصيرة ويغنه ، وهذا أوانُ الشروع في المقصود ، متوكلاً على فائض الخير والجود.
He was called 'Iman', which the scholars interpreted as belief and submission, so they called him 'Sheikh al-Taslim'. He died as a martyr in the year 738 AH. The reason for his killing: a large group of Turks in that region were engaged in oppression and misguidance, abandoning the rulings of Shari'ah. So Sheikh Nizam al-Din issued a fatwa declaring them disbelievers. When they learned of it, they gathered the army and reached Herat. The Sultan did not have the power to resist them, so he fortified himself in a fortress. They sent a messenger to him saying: 'Our only aim in igniting the fire of war is to kill the one who declared us disbelievers, nothing else. If the objective is to protect the people of Herat, their wealth, and their children, then let them hand over that mufti to us.' When the situation became dire for the people of Herat and they were in distress and confusion, they issued a fatwa that it is permissible to bear a specific harm to repel a general harm. So they sent him to the Sheikh. He realized their intention, descended from the pulpit, performed ritual washing, put on his best clothes, and left the city. The oppressors took him and killed him. Ended, summarized. And similarly in Rawdat al-Jannat fi Fada'il Harat, but there it mentions his death in Dhul-Qa'dah 737 AH. Ibn Hubba Fasih al-Din al-Harawi said in his commentary on al-Wiqaya in the Book of Zakat: Sadr al-Shari'ah said: 'Look at this one who inserted another pillar into Iman, how he clings to this narration, and thus permitted the rulers of Herat to take 'ushr and zakat in the known manner; rather he made it obligatory upon them, and ruled that whoever denies it is a disbeliever.' The known manner is that they incite assistants to take from the land produce multiplied multiples, imposing high values on the owners, and take them by force and compulsion, and spend them as is the custom of the extravagant. In this he referred to my maternal grandfather, the great Sheikh of Islam, the Imam of the eminent Imams in the world, the reviver of the rituals of the religion among the nations, the eliminator of the ferocity of the beasts of innovation and the effects of oppression, the blessed martyr Nizam al-Milla wa al-Shari'ah wa al-Taqwa wa al-Din, Abd al-Rahim, known among the people of Islam as Sheikh al-Taslim... Then he responded to the objections of Sadr al-Shari'ah, supported his grandfather, and verified his lofty statements. You will come across it in its place, God willing. This is the end of the discussion at this juncture. I have mentioned in Muqaddimat al-Sa'ayah the conditions of the books from which Sadr al-Shari'ah transmitted the issues and others, and the conditions of lineages, tribes, and places mentioned in Sharh al-Wiqaya, and the biographies of the commentators of Mukhtasar al-Wiqaya. I saw that deleting them here is more appropriate seeking brevity to the extent needed, and leaving what is unnecessary. You will see in the Hashiyah in every place what is suitable, in a manner that benefits the student and suffices him. This is the time to begin the intended subject, relying on the abundant goodness and generosity.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the martyrdom of Sheikh Nizam al-Din, a Hanafi scholar, and the defense of his grandfather by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The author recounts how Sheikh Nizam al-Din issued a fatwa of kufr against oppressive Turks, leading to his martyrdom after the people of Herat, under duress, applied the legal principle that a specific harm may be borne to avert a general harm. The author then addresses criticism from Sadr al-Shari'ah, who accused the grandfather of adding another pillar to iman and using a weak narration to justify the rulers' oppressive collection of 'ushr and zakat. The author defends his grandfather—a revered scholar known as Sheikh al-Taslim—and promises to respond fully later in the text. This section illustrates the Hanafi approach to necessity (darurah) and the complex interplay between fiqh, politics, and personal sacrifice.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman): In this passage, 'iman' is defined by scholars as 'tasdiq' (belief/affirmation) and 'taslim' (submission).
الضرر الخاص (al-darar al-khass): A harm affecting a specific individual or group, as opposed to a general harm affecting the whole community.
الضرر العام (al-darar al-'amm): A widespread harm affecting the larger community or the public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the people of Herat hand over Sheikh Nizam al-Din?
A) Because they hated him
B) Because they applied the principle that a specific harm is borne to repel a general harm
C) Because the Sultan ordered it
D) Because the Sheikh asked them to

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim of al-darar al-khass yuhtamalu li-daf' al-darar al-'amm permits sacrificing a specific good to protect a greater communal good, but its application must be made by qualified scholars in genuine necessity.

The Iman Controversy: Defending Sheikh al-Taslim from Sadr al-Shari'ah's Objections
Theological controversy over the definition of iman and its impact on fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of iman as tasdiq and taslim, and evaluate the argument that inserting an additional pillar affects the ruling on tax collection.

Core Idea
Defining iman has direct fiqh consequences—adding a pillar could justify oppressive taxation; the author defends his grandfather's position.

This lesson focuses on the latter part of the passage where the author defends his grandfather, Sheikh Nizam al-Din, against criticism from Sadr al-Shari'ah. Sadr accused the grandfather of inserting another pillar into the definition of iman, thereby permitting rulers to collect 'ushr and zakat unjustly. We will examine the Hanafi definition of iman (tasdiq and taslim), the nature of the accusation, and the author's defense. This case highlights the interplay between theology and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation
Read where Sadr al-Shari'ah said the grandfather inserted another pillar into iman. What is the alleged consequence?
He permitted the oppressive collection of 'ushr and zakat.
2
Recall the Hanafi definition of iman
Hanafis define iman as affirmation with the heart and confession with the tongue. Actions are not part of iman. Compare this to the text's mention of 'tasdiq wa taslim'.
3
Analyze the link between iman definition and tax collection
Why would adding a pillar affect tax collection? Consider the term 'ruknan akhar' (another pillar). How could that justify the rulers' actions?
The author says his grandfather did not add a pillar; the objection is unfounded.
4
Evaluate the author's defense
The author promises to respond in detail later. For now, note how he praises his grandfather as a reviver of the religion. What does this indicate about the seriousness of the accusation?

The known manner is that they incite assistants to take from the land produce multiplied multiples, imposing high values on the owners, and take them by force and compulsion, and spend them as is the custom of the extravagant. In this he referred to my maternal grandfather, the great Sheikh of Islam, the Imam of the eminent Imams in the world, the reviver of the rituals of the religion among the nations, the eliminator of the ferocity of the beasts of innovation and the effects of oppression, the blessed martyr Nizam al-Milla wa al-Shari'ah wa al-Taqwa wa al-Din, Abd al-Rahim, known among the people of Islam as Sheikh al-Taslim... Then he responded to the objections of Sadr al-Shari'ah, supported his grandfather, and verified his lofty statements. You will come across it in its place, God willing. This is the end of the discussion at this juncture. I have mentioned in Muqaddimat al-Sa'ayah the conditions of the books from which Sadr al-Shari'ah transmitted the issues and others, and the conditions of lineages, tribes, and places mentioned in Sharh al-Wiqaya, and the biographies of the commentators of Mukhtasar al-Wiqaya. I saw that deleting them here is more appropriate seeking brevity to the extent needed, and leaving what is unnecessary. You will see in the Hashiyah in every place what is suitable, in a manner that benefits the student and suffices him. This is the time to begin the intended subject, relying on the abundant goodness and generosity.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the martyrdom of Sheikh Nizam al-Din, a Hanafi scholar, and the defense of his grandfather by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The author recounts how Sheikh Nizam al-Din issued a fatwa of kufr against oppressive Turks, leading to his martyrdom after the people of Herat, under duress, applied the legal principle that a specific harm may be borne to avert a general harm. The author then addresses criticism from Sadr al-Shari'ah, who accused the grandfather of adding another pillar to iman and using a weak narration to justify the rulers' oppressive collection of 'ushr and zakat. The author defends his grandfather—a revered scholar known as Sheikh al-Taslim—and promises to respond fully later in the text. This section illustrates the Hanafi approach to necessity (darurah) and the complex interplay between fiqh, politics, and personal sacrifice.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman): In this passage, 'iman' is defined by scholars as 'tasdiq' (belief/affirmation) and 'taslim' (submission).
الضرر الخاص (al-darar al-khass): A harm affecting a specific individual or group, as opposed to a general harm affecting the whole community.
الضرر العام (al-darar al-'amm): A widespread harm affecting the larger community or the public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Sadr al-Shari'ah accuse the grandfather of?
A) Declaring the Turks disbelievers incorrectly
B) Inserting another pillar into the definition of iman
C) Ruling that zakat is not obligatory
D) Abandoning the Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
The definition of iman has direct fiqh consequences; an imputation of altering that definition can have serious legal implications.

The Pronoun 'Hādhā' and Mental Presence in Legal Interpretation
Linguistic and theological clarification of the pronoun 'hādhā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic usage of 'hādhā' for mental presence and apply it to interpret a hadith correctly, avoiding an erroneous inference of physical presence.

Core Idea
The pronoun 'hādhā' can refer to something mentally present, not physically – a key rule in interpreting texts.

This passage explains a linguistic rule: the demonstrative pronoun 'hādhā' (this) is often used to refer to something present in the mind, even if not physically there. This is crucial for understanding certain Quranic verses and hadith, especially the hadith of the grave questioning, where the angels ask about 'this man' referring to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Some mistakenly deduced that the Prophet's physical body is in every believer's grave. The author refutes this, citing Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani that this is not established. Understanding this rule prevents misinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Basic and Extended Usage
Recognize that 'hādhā' primarily points to physically present objects, but is extended to refer to something mentally present, treating the abstract as perceptible.
This is a common rhetorical device in Arabic called 'tanẓīl al-maʿqūl manzilat al-maḥsūs'.
2
Examine Quranic Examples
Look at the Quranic verses cited: 'That Book' (2:2), 'this blessed Book' (6:92), 'this blessed Reminder' (21:50). The Quran is not physically present at the time of utterance, yet it is referred to as if present.
3
Apply to the Hadith of the Grave
In the hadith, the angels ask: 'What did you say about this man?' referring to the Prophet, who is not physically present in the grave. The use of 'hādhā' indicates mental presence, not physical.
The hadith is transmitted by al-Tirmidhi, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn Abi al-Dunya.
4
Refute the Misinterpretation
Some scholars deduced that the Prophet's physical form (timthāl muḥammadī) is present in every believer's grave. This is an incorrect inference because the pronoun does not require physical presence.
Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani explicitly states that this conclusion is not established (thābit).
5
Confirm the Correct Principle
The correct principle is that 'hādhā' can refer to something mentally present. Therefore, no physical presence is implied. This is supported by numerous examples in Arabic.
The author emphasizes that the mental presence is due to the clarity and distinction of the referent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا [١] قوله : هذا ؛ هو وإن كان موضوعاً للمشار إليه الموجود في الخارج المحسوس ، لكن كثيراً ما يشار به إلى الحاضر في الذهن تنزيلاً للمعقول منزلة المحسوس، وتنبيهاً على كمال تمييزه ، ومنه قوله وجل : ذَلِكَ الْكِتَبُ لَا رَيْبَ فِيهِ ) ، وقوله : وَهَذَا كتبُ أَنزَلْتَهُ مُبَارَك (٢) ، وقوله لان : في وَهَذَا ذِكْرٌ مُبَارَكُ أَنزَلَتَهُ } () (۲) ونظائره في القرآن والحديث وكلمات العرب كثيرة، ومنه ما ورد في رواية الترمذي والبيهقي وابن أبي الدنيا (4) وغيرهم في حديث سؤال منكر ونكير في القبر 6 (0) فيقولان له: ما كنت تقول في هذا الرجل ؟ فيقول المؤمن : هو عبد الله ورسوله فالإشارة بهذا فيه ليس إلا إلى الحاضر في الذهن، ومن استنبط منه حضور التمثال المحمدي أو نفس ذاته في قبر كل مؤمن فقد غفل، وقد صرح الحافظ ابن حجر العسقلاني كما نقله عنها السيوطي في «شرح الصدور في أحوال الموتى والقبور» بأنّ ذلك ليس بثابت.
This [1] His statement: 'hādhā' (this). Although it is used for pointing to something physically present and perceptible, frequently it is used to refer to something present in the mind, treating the abstract as if it were perceptible, and to indicate its complete distinctness. From this is His statement, the Mighty and Sublime: 'That Book, there is no doubt in it' (2:2), and His statement: 'And this is a Book which We have sent down, blessed' (6:92), and His statement: 'And this is a blessed Reminder which We have sent down' (21:50), and similar examples in the Quran, hadith, and the words of the Arabs are many. Among them is that which occurs in the narration of al-Tirmidhi, al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Abi al-Dunya (4), and others in the hadith of the questioning by Munkar and Nakir in the grave: they say to him: 'What did you used to say about this man?' So the believer says: 'He is the servant of Allah and His messenger.' Therefore, the reference with 'hādhā' here is only to what is present in the mind. Whoever deduces from it the presence of the physical form of Muhammad or his very essence in the grave of every believer has become heedless. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani, as quoted by al-Suyuti in 'Sharh al-Sudūr fī Ahwāl al-Mawtā wa al-Qubūr', stated clearly that this is not established.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the usage of the demonstrative pronoun 'hādhā' (this). While it is normally used for pointing to a physically present object, it is frequently used in Arabic to refer to something mentally present, as in the Quranic verses where 'dhālika' and 'hādhā' are used for the Quran which is not physically present at the moment of revelation. The author uses the hadith of the grave questioning, where the angels ask 'what did you say about this man?' referring to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), who is not physically present in the grave. The author then refutes a misinterpretation by some who claim that the Prophet's physical body is in the grave of every believer, citing Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani that this is not established.

Key Terms
حاضر في الذهن (hāḍir fī al-dhihn): Something present in the mind, not physically present, yet referred to as if it were present.
تمثال محمدي (timthāl muḥammadī): The physical form or image of the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the word 'hādhā' in the hadith of Munkar and Nakir not indicate the physical presence of the Prophet in every believer's grave?
A) Because the Prophet is not in any grave
B) Because 'hādhā' can refer to something present in the mind, not physically
C) Because the hadith is weak
D) Because the angels asked in a different language

Key Takeaway
The demonstrative pronoun 'hādhā' commonly indicates mental presence, not necessarily physical presence, a crucial rule for correct interpretation of Islamic texts.

Definition of Fiqh in Hanafi Custom
Analysis of various definitions of fiqh and criteria for being a faqih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi technical definition of fiqh (usul) from the customary definition (furū‘) and explain the legal implications for who qualifies as a faqih.

Core Idea
Fiqh is the knowledge of practical rulings; in customary usage, it means the branches themselves.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of fiqh (الفقه) as presented by Hanafi scholars. The passage contrasts the usul (principles) definition, which restricts fiqh to knowledge of rulings from their evidences, with the furu' (branches) definition, which in juristic custom treats fiqh as the rulings themselves. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting texts that use the term 'fiqh' and for knowing who qualifies as a faqih in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiple definitions
List the three definitions of fiqh mentioned in the passage: as a science of conditions, as memorization of branches (minimum three), and as a collection of branches.
These definitions are cited from al-Khadimi, Radd al-Muhtar, and al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm.
2
Note the refutation of al-Lakhnawi
The passage rejects al-Lakhnawi's claim that fiqh is only for mujtahids. Observe the evidence given: the customary usage of 'fuqaha’' in endowments and bequests includes non-mujtahids.
Al-Lakhnawi's view is based on the usul definition, but the passage argues that custom overrides.
3
Distinguish usul from furu' definitions
Ibn Nujaym clarifies: in usul al-fiqh, fiqh is knowledge of rulings from evidences, and a faqih is only a mujtahid. But in the custom of jurists, fiqh means the branches/issues themselves, and a memorizer of rulings is called a faqih.
This distinction is key to interpreting legal documents like waqf and wasiyyah.
4
Understand the role of custom
The passage states that the literal meaning (faqih = mujtahid) is abandoned due to customary usage. Custom becomes the determinant of what 'faqih' means in practice.
This is a core Hanafi principle: al-haqiqah al-‘urfiyyah can override al-haqiqah al-lughawiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على تعريف الفقهاء بأنه علم يبحث فيه عن أحوال الأعمال من حيث الحل والحرمة والفساد والصحة كما في حاشية الخادمي على الدرر (ص) (۳) ، أو حفظ الفروع وأقله ثلاث كما في رد المحتار) (۱) : (٢٦) ، أو مجموعة من الفروع كما في المدخل الفقهي العام» (١ : ٥٥) ، فإن كلام اللكنوي لا يستقيم ؛ لأنه الفقه هو الفروع والمسائل، والعالم بـهـا يكون فقيهاً في مصطلح الفقهاء. دلائلها. ، قال ابن نجيم في البحر (۱ : ۷ : فالحاصل أن الفقه في الأصول : علم الأحكام من فليس الفقيه إلا المجتهد عندهم، وإطلاقه على المقلد الحافظ للمسائل مجاز، وهو حقيقة في عرف الفقهاء بدليل انصراف الوقف، والوصية للفقهاء إليهم، وأقله ثلاثة أحكام، كما في «المنتقى»، وذكر في «التحرير» أن الشائع إطلاقه على من يحفظ الفروع مطلقاً، سواء كانت بدلائلها أم لا». ، يعني لكن صرح ! الأصوليون بأن الحقيقة تترك بدلالة العادة، وحينئذ ينصرف كلام الواقف الموصي للفقهاء إلى ما هو المتعارف في زمنه ؛ لأنه حقيقة كلامه العرفية، فتترك به الحقيقة الأصلية، ويكون حقيقة في عرف الفقهاء. كذا في رد المحتار»(۱ : ۳۷۸). وتكلموا في المقدار الأدنى الذي يجب أن يحفظه الشخص حتى يطلق عليه لقب : الفقيه ; وانتهوا إلى أن هذا متروك للعرف، ونستطيع أن نقرر أن عرفنا الآن لا يطلق لقب : فقيه إلا على من يعرف موطن الحكم من أبواب الفقه المتناثرة بحيث يسهل عليه الرجوع إليه. كما في الموسوعة الفقهية الكويتية» (١) : ١٤)، وغيرها.
As for the definition of the jurists that it is a science that investigates the conditions of actions in terms of permissibility, prohibition, invalidity, and validity, as in the footnote of al-Khadimi on al-Durar (p. 3), or the memorization of branches and its minimum is three as in Radd al-Muhtar (1:26), or a collection of branches as in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm (1:55), then the statement of al-Lakhnawi is not sound, because fiqh is the branches and issues, and the one who knows them is a faqih in the terminology of the jurists. Their evidences. Ibn Nujaym said in al-Bahr (1:7): 'In short, fiqh in the principles is the knowledge of rulings, and the faqih is only the mujtahid according to them, and its application to the muqallid who memorizes issues is metaphorical, and it is literal in the custom of the jurists, as evidenced by the fact that endowment and bequest to fuqaha’ are directed to them, and its minimum is three rulings, as in al-Muntaqa.' It is mentioned in al-Tahrir that the common usage is to apply it to anyone who memorizes the branches absolutely, whether with their evidences or not. Then the usuliyyun explicitly stated that the literal meaning is abandoned due to customary usage, and then the speech of the endower or testator referring to fuqaha’ is understood according to what is customary in his time, because it is the customary reality of his speech, so the original literal meaning is abandoned, and it becomes literal in the custom of the jurists. This is in Radd al-Muhtar (1:378). They discussed the minimum amount that a person must memorize to be called a faqih, and they concluded that this is left to custom. We can say that our custom now does not apply the title 'faqih' except to one who knows the location of the ruling from the scattered chapters of fiqh in such a way that it is easy for him to refer to it. As in al-Mawsu‘a al-Fiqhiyya al-Kuwaytiyya (1:14) and others.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of fiqh and who qualifies as a faqih. It presents multiple definitions: fiqh as a science of legal conditions (halal, haram, etc.), as memorization of branches (minimum three), or as a collection of branches. The author refutes al-Lakhnawi’s view that fiqh is only for mujtahids, stating that in the custom of fuqaha’, one who knows the rulings (even as a muqallid) is called a faqih. Ibn Nujaym notes that in usul al-fiqh, a faqih is only a mujtahid, but in common juristic usage, even a memorizer of rulings is called faqih, as evidenced by endowments and bequests to 'fuqaha’' which include non-mujtahids. The minimum number of rulings required is debated but ultimately left to custom; the contemporary custom requires knowing where to find the ruling rather than memorizing a specific number.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of practical legal rulings (furū‘); in this passage, it is equated with the branches and issues themselves.
الفقيه (al-faqīh): One who knows the furū‘ (branches), either as a mujtahid or as a muqallid who memorizes rulings. In custom, a muqallid is also called faqih.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowledge of the evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that the term 'faqih' in juristic custom includes muqallids?
A) The definition of al-Khadimi
B) The mention in al-Muntaqa
C) The practice of endowments and bequests to ‘fuqaha’’
D) The conclusion of the contemporary encyclopedia

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in Hanafi custom means the branches themselves, and a faqih includes both mujtahids and muqallids who know the rulings.

The Minimum to Be a Faqih: Custom Over Fixed Number
Analysis of various definitions of fiqh and criteria for being a faqih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minimum number of rulings for a faqih is left to custom and apply this principle to contemporary contexts.

Core Idea
There is no fixed minimum to be called a faqih; custom determines the qualification.

In this lesson, we focus on the debate over the minimum number of rulings a person must know to be called a faqih. The passage shows that scholars discussed a possible minimum of three but ultimately left it to customary practice. Today, knowing where to find rulings is considered sufficient. This highlights the role of custom (urf) in defining legal competence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed minimum
The passage mentions that in al-Muntaqa, a minimum of three rulings is suggested. Note this as one opinion.
Also cited in Radd al-Muhtar.
2
Note the conclusion of the scholars
The passage explicitly states: 'they concluded that this is left to custom.' There is no fixed number.
This is a key principle: the minimum is not scripturally defined.
3
Understand the rationale: custom overrides literal meaning
The passage earlier explained that the literal meaning of 'faqih' (mujtahid) is abandoned due to customary usage. Similarly, the minimum is determined by what is customary in each time and place.
Reference the earlier discussion of al-haqiqah al-‘urfiyyah.
4
Apply to contemporary custom
The passage quotes the Kuwaiti Encyclopedia: today, a faqih is one who can locate the ruling in the chapters of fiqh easily. This is the current custom.
Notice the shift from memorization to accessibility.

Source Text

Reasons for Opening with the Verse of Wudu
Methodological discussion on why the verse of wudu was chosen to open the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons the author of al-Wiqayah began with the verse of wudu and evaluate the objection raised against the blessing reason.

Core Idea
The verse of wudu provides both legal evidence and a profound blessing through its meaning of purification.

Why does a respected Hanafi fiqh text open with a verse about wudu? In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator explains that the author had two purposes: first, to ground the rulings that follow in a clear legal evidence, and second, seeking blessing (tayammun). When an objection arises — that any verse could bring blessing — the commentator refines the argument, showing that the blessing is not only verbal but lies in the meaning of the verse itself. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and helps you appreciate the pedagogical wisdom behind arranging a fiqh text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons
Read the first few sentences of the Arabic passage. The commentator explicitly states two reasons: (1) a ruling must be based on a legal evidence, so he begins with the verse as evidence; (2) for blessing (tayammun). List them in your own words.
The first reason is more straightforward; the second requires you to understand the concept of tayammun.
2
Understand the objection
The commentator presents an objection: if the goal is blessing, any verse could bring it, so why this specific verse? Also, blessing does not require opening with it. Write the objection clearly.
The objection challenges the sufficiency of the blessing reason alone.
3
Analyze the initial response
The initial response claims that the blessing is intended for beginning speech, not absolute blessing. However, the commentator finds this weak. Why is it weak? Because it doesn't address why this verse is special.
The weakness is that it restricts blessing to opening but doesn't justify this verse.
4
Recognize the preferred answer
The preferred answer distinguishes between verbal blessing (which any verse can provide) and meaning-based blessing, which is unique to this verse because it discusses purification from impurities, symbolically cleansing the learner from sins. Note this nuance.
This answer addresses both the objection and deepens the understanding of tayammun.
5
Apply to other contexts
Consider how this reasoning applies when a scholar chooses a verse or hadith to begin a chapter. Why might you choose a specific text over others? Reflect on the balance between evidence and blessing.
This step encourages you to think like a compiler of fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢. ومنها : إن الحكم إنما يكون مقبولاً إذا ثبت عن دليل شرعي؛ إذ لا مدخل للرأي في الأحكام، فابتدأ بالآية التي هي دليلُ ما يذكر بعدها، ليكون الحكم في أول ورودِهِ على ذهن المتعلم مقبولاً عنده... ومنها : ما ذكره الشارح بقوله: تيمناً ; أي للتبرك. ومنها ما ذكره بقوله : ولأنّ...الخ. ويرد على الدليل الأول : أنّ التيمن يحصل لغير هذه الآية أيضاً، فالدليل غير تام، فإنّ الدعوى إنما هي الافتتاح بهذه الآية. وأيضاً : التيمن لا يتوقف على الافتتاح، فإنه لو ذكره في الوسط أيضاً لحصل التيمن. والجواب : أنّ المراد التيمن في بدء الكلام لا المطلق، والتيمن دليل للافتتاح المطلق لا المقيد، وفيه ضعف ظاهر، والأولى أن يقال : إنّ التنوين في التيمن للنوعية، والمراد التيمن باللفظ والمعنى جميعاً، والتيمّن اللفظي وإن كان يحصل لغيرها أيضاً، لكن المعنوي خاص بها، لما فيه من معنى الغسل المطهر عن النجاسات الحقيقة والحكميّة، فيتوسل بذكره إلى أن يجعل مغسولاً من الذنوب الدنيئة.
2. Among them: that a ruling is only acceptable when established by a legal evidence, for there is no room for opinion in rulings. So he began with the verse that is the evidence for what follows, so that the ruling upon its first occurrence in the learner's mind may be acceptable to him... Among them: what the commentator mentioned in his words: 'tayammunan' i.e. for blessing. Among them: what he mentioned in his words: 'and because...' etc. An objection is raised against the first evidence: that blessing is also obtained by other than this verse, so the evidence is incomplete, for the claim is only about opening with this verse. Also: blessing does not depend on opening, for if he mentioned it in the middle, blessing would also be obtained. The answer: what is meant is blessing in beginning speech, not absolute blessing, and tayammun is evidence for absolute opening, not restricted opening. There is obvious weakness in this. Better it is said: the tanwin in 'tayammun' is for specifying the type, and what is meant is blessing in both word and meaning. Verbal blessing can be obtained by other verses too, but the meaning-based blessing is specific to this verse, because it contains the meaning of washing that purifies from real and figurative impurities, so by mentioning it one seeks to be washed from base sins.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion from the sharh of al-Wiqayah. It explains why the author (al-Mahbubi) began the book with the verse of wudu (5:6) rather than stating the rulings directly. Two reasons are given: first, that a ruling must be based on evidence, and the verse is the evidence for the subsequent rulings; second, for blessing (tayammun). Then an objection is raised: blessing could be obtained by starting with any verse, so why this particular one? Also, blessing doesn't require starting with it. The commentator provides an answer: the intended blessing is specifically in beginning one's speech, not absolute blessing. However, the commentator finds this answer weak and offers a better one: the blessing is of two kinds—verbal and meaning-based. The meaning-based blessing is unique to this verse because it mentions purification from physical and spiritual impurities, thereby associating the book with the concept of cleansing from sins. Thus, starting with this verse is more fitting.

Key Terms
التيمن (al-tayammun): Seeking blessing (tabarruk) by beginning with something auspicious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred answer to the objection that blessing could be obtained by starting with any verse?
A) The objection is invalid because the author chose this verse arbitrarily.
B) The tayammun is both verbal and meaning-based; the meaning-based blessing is specific to this verse.
C) The author intended only absolute blessing, not specific to this verse.
D) The tayammun is obtained only by starting the entire book with this verse.

Key Takeaway
The author began with the verse of wudu both to provide legal evidence for the rulings and to seek a profound meaning-based blessing associated with purification.
