When the Wipe Period Begins and What Nullifies It
Ruling on nullifiers and timing dispute with al-Hasan and Ahmad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the start of the wipe period over khuffs, explain the evidence from the hadith, and distinguish it from the views of al-Hasan al-Basri and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, including the practical consequence.

Core Idea
The wipe period begins from the first hadath, not from wearing or first wudu.

Wiping over khuffs is a concession (rukhsah) that allows one to wipe the feet instead of washing them during wudu, but it is valid only within a specific time. The precise starting point of that time is disputed among scholars. In this passage, the author clarifies the Hanafi position and contrasts it with the views of al-Hasan al-Basri and Imam Ahmad, providing a practical example to illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is wiping over khuffs. The disputed ruling is from which moment the permissible duration begins. Determine what is being ruled upon.
The ruling itself (permissibility of wiping within a period) is agreed upon; only the start time is contested.
2
State the Hanafi position and its dalil
The Hanafi school holds that the period begins from the time of hadath (the first event that nullifies wudu after wearing). The primary evidence is the hadith specifying the duration (one day for resident, three for traveler), and the reasoning that the need for wiping arises only after hadath.
The hadith is quoted: 'يَمْسَحُ المُقِيمُ يَوْمَاً وَلَيْلَة، والمُسَافِرُ ثَلاثَةَ أَيَّامٍ'.
3
Identify the opposing positions and their proofs
Al-Hasan al-Basri counts from the time of wearing the khuff; Imam Ahmad counts from the time of the first wudu (at morning). Each relies on a different understanding of when the concession is applicable.
Their proofs are based on their own interpretations of the hadith and the concept of need.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response emphasizes that before hadath, there is no need to wipe, so the period must begin when the need arises. Additional arguments: the khuff prevents the hadath from reaching the foot only after hadath, and the license (rukhsah) is intended for that time.
5
Apply to the example and note the thamarah al-khilaf
In the given example (wudu at dawn, hadath after sunrise, wipe at noon), the Hanafi view allows wiping until sunrise the next day; al-Hasan until noon the next day; Ahmad until dawn the next day. This practical difference shows the real-world impact of the dispute.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the actual time window during which wiping is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة / المسح على الخفين وينقضه : ناقض الوضوء لان قوله : «يَمْسَحُ المُقِيمُ يَوْمَاً وَلَيْلَة ، والمُسَافِرُ ثلاثة أيام الحديث، أفاد جواز المسح في المدَّة المذكورة، وقبل الحَدَثِ لا احتياج إلى المسح ، فالزمان الذي يُحتاج فيه إلى المسح ، وهو من وقتِ الحدثِ) مقدَّرٌ بالمقدار المذكور ( وينقضُهُ : ناقض الوضوء من حين يمسح بعد الحدث، وعن الحسن البصري من حين اللبس. فمن توضأ عند طلوع الفجر وليس الخف وأحدث بعد طلوع الشمس فتوضأ ومسح بعد الزوال فعلى قول الجمهور يجوز له المسح إلى طلوع الشمس من اليوم الثاني، وإلى طلوع الفجر عند أحمد، وإلى زوال اليوم الثاني عند الحسن، والبسط في دلائل المذاهب مفوض إلى «السعاية». قوله : يمسح... الخ ؛ أخرجه الطبراني من حديث البراء، وأبو نعيم من حديث مالك بن سعد، ومسلم من حديث علي وأبو داود من حديث خزيمة، وابن أبي شيبة من حديث عمر، والترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجة له وغيرهم من حديث صفوان، وقد بسط الكلام فيه الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «تخريج أحاديث الهداية». قوله : وهو من وقت الحدث؛ لأنه الزمان الذي وجد فيه سبب وجوب الطهارة وانتقاض الطهارة السابقة. قوله : بالمقدار المذكور؛ لكونه وقت وجود السبب، وأيضاً هو وقت منع الخف سراية الحدث إلى القدم، وأيضاً هو وقت وجود الرخصة، فكان أحق بالاعتبار من وقت اللبس ووقت الطهارة.
The book of purification / Wiping over leather socks: And it is nullified by what nullifies wudu. Because his statement: 'The resident wipes for a day and a night, and the traveler for three days' — the hadith indicates the permissibility of wiping for the mentioned duration. Before the hadath, there is no need for wiping. So the time when wiping is needed, which is from the time of hadath, is determined by the mentioned amount. And it is nullified by what nullifies wudu from the moment one wipes after the hadath. And from Hasan al-Basri: from the time of wearing. So if one performs wudu at dawn and wears the khuff, then has a hadath after sunrise, then performs wudu and wipes after noon, then according to the majority, it is permissible for him to wipe until sunrise of the second day; according to Ahmad, until dawn; according to al-Hasan, until noon of the second day. Elaboration on the proofs of the schools is delegated to 'al-Sa'ayah'. He said: 'He wipes... etc.'; it was narrated by al-Tabarani from al-Bara', by Abu Nu'aym from Malik ibn Sa'd, by Muslim from 'Ali, by Abu Dawud from Khuzaymah, by Ibn Abi Shaybah from 'Umar, by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah and others from Safwan. Al-Zayla'i has elaborated on it in 'Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah'. He said: 'It is from the time of hadath because it is the time when the cause for the obligation of purity and the nullification of previous purity occurred.' He said: 'By the mentioned amount because it is the time of the cause's existence, and also it is the time when the khuff prevents the hadath from reaching the foot, and also it is the time of the license, so it is more deserving of consideration than the time of wearing and the time of wudu.'
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the starting point of the wipe period and a ruling on what nullifies the wipe. The author states that the wipe is nullified by anything that nullifies wudu, which is agreed upon. The dispute is about from when to count the duration: the Hanafi position, following the majority, counts from the time of hadath (the event that necessitates wudu), because that is the time when wiping becomes needed. Al-Hasan al-Basri counts from the time of wearing the khuff, and Imam Ahmad counts from the time of the first wudu (at dawn). The passage provides a practical example: if one wears the khuff after wudu at dawn, has a hadath after sunrise, then wipes at noon, the Hanafi view allows wiping until sunrise of the next day, while others differ. The author then gives the hadith evidence for the duration and explains why the time of hadath is the most appropriate starting point: it is the cause for obligation, the time when the khuff prevents the hadath, and the time of the license.

Key Terms
المسح على الخفين (al-masḥ 'alā al-khuffayn): Wiping over leather socks as a substitute for washing the feet in wudu, a concession (rukhsah) for travelers and residents within a specified time.
ناقض الوضوء (nāqiḍ al-wuḍū’): Anything that nullifies wudu, such as passing wind, urine, stool, deep sleep, etc. These also nullify the license of wiping, requiring renewal of wudu and potentially a new wipe if within the period.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): An event that nullifies wudu, such as passing wind, urine, etc. In the context of wiping, it is the event that triggers the need to use the license of wiping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from which moment does the permissible duration of wiping over khuffs begin?
A) From the moment of wearing the khuffs.
B) From the time of the first hadath after wearing.
C) From the time of the first wudu after wearing.
D) From the time of the first wipe.

Key Takeaway
The permissible period for wiping over khuffs begins from the first hadath after wearing, not from the time of wearing or the initial wudu.

Removing One Sock Nullifies the Wipe on Both
Ruling on removing one khuff and its effect on the wipe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on removing one khuff and explain the legal reasoning based on the impossibility of combining washing and wiping.

Core Idea
Removing one khuff nullifies the wipe on both feet because washing one foot necessitates washing the other in the same wudu.

This passage clarifies why the singular form 'al-khuff' is used in the text: to emphasize that even the removal of a single sock from one foot nullifies the entire wipe. The underlying logic is that once a foot becomes exposed, it must be washed in wudu, and combining washing on one foot with wiping on the other is not legally permissible. Therefore, the previous wipe on both feet becomes invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the legal consequence is. The act is removing one khuff from one foot. The ruling: it nullifies the wipe on both feet, making it necessary to wash both feet in the next wudu.
The text uses the singular 'al-khuff' to include the case of a single sock.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Why does removing one sock nullify the wipe on the other foot? Because if you remove one sock, that foot must be washed in wudu. Then you cannot wipe the other foot while washing the first, as Islamic law does not permit combining washing and wiping in the same duty (the same wudu). Hence the wipe on the other foot is also nullified.
This is a 'legal impossibility' (ta'azzur shar'i), not a physical one.
3
Examine the dalil
The text cites a hadith from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar: he removed both khuffs and washed his feet without renewing wudu. This shows that removal brings back the obligation of washing. By analogy, if removing one sock also obligates washing, it leads to the same consequence.
The hadith is ahad, giving zanni certainty.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that removing one sock only affects that foot; you wash that foot and continue wiping on the other. The Hanafi school rejects this due to the legal impossibility of mixing washing and wiping in one wudu.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi in the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونزع الخف، ذكر لفظ الواحد، ولم يقل: نزع الخفين؛ ليفيد أن نزع أحدهما ناقض، فإنه إذا نزع أحدهما وجب غسل إحدى الرجلين، فوجب غسل الأخرى، إذ لا جمع بين الغسل والمسح، وكذا إن دخل الماء أحد خفيه حتى صار جميع الرجل مغسولاً فانتقض فيجب تجديد المسح عند تجديد الوضوء عند بقاء المدة. قوله: ونزع الخف؛ لما روي عن عبد الله بن عمر أنه كان في غزوة فنزع خفيه وغسل قدميه ولم يعد الوضوء، وهكذا روي عن غيره من أصحاب رسول الله فعلم أن الحدث إنما يزول بالمسح مؤقتاً لا مؤبداً. كذا في «حواشي الهداية» لملا الهداد الجونفوري. قوله: وجب غسل إحدى الرجلين؛ وهو الذي نزعه منه؛ لسراية الحدث إلى القدم، حيث زال الخف المانع، فإن الخف عهد شرعاً مانعاً عن سراية الحدث إلى ما فيه. قوله: فوجب؛ قال صاحب الهداية: «وكذا نزع أحدهما؛ لتعذر الجمع بين الغسل والمسح في وظيفة واحدة». انتهى قال الجونفوري في «حواشيه»: «يعني المسح مع الغسل لم يشرع؛ إذ المسح طهارة غير معقولة، فيقتصر على مورد الشرع، فالمراد بالتعذر التعذر الشرعي، أو المراد أنه يتعذر حكم الجمع بينهما. قوله: إذ لا جمع؛ يعني لا جمع في الشرع بين الغسل والمسح: أي في وظيفة واحدة، وأما عند تعدد الوظيفة فالجمع موجود؛ كغسل الوجه واليدين والرجلين مع مسح الرأس في الوضوء، فلو مسح على الخف الواحدة، وغسل إحدى الرجلين لزم الجمع بينهما في وظيفة واحدة متعلقة بالرجلين. قوله: جميع الرجل مغسولاً؛ قال قاضي خان في «فتاواه»: «ماسح الخف إذا دخل الماء خفيه فغسل رجليه انتقض وضوؤه
And removing the khuff: he mentioned the word in singular, and did not say 'removing the two khuffs' to indicate that removing one of them is a nullifier. Because if he removes one, it becomes obligatory to wash one foot, then it becomes obligatory to wash the other, since there is no combining between washing and wiping. Likewise, if water enters one of his khuffs until the entire foot becomes washed, then it is nullified, so it becomes obligatory to renew the wipe when renewing wudu if the period remains. He said: 'And removing the khuff' because it is narrated from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar that he was on a military expedition and he removed his two khuffs and washed his feet and did not repeat wudu. Thus it is also narrated from other Companions of the Messenger of Allah, so it is known that hadath is only removed by wiping temporarily, not permanently. Likewise in 'Hawashi al-Hidayah' by Mulla al-Hidad al-Jawnpuri. He said: 'It becomes obligatory to wash one foot' which is the one from which he removed the khuff, because the hadath reaches the foot when the preventing khuff is removed, for the khuff is known by law as a preventer of the hadath reaching what is inside it. He said: 'Then it becomes obligatory' – the author of al-Hidayah said: 'Likewise, removing one of them is obligatory because it is impossible to combine washing and wiping in one duty.' End. Al-Jawnpuri said in his Hawashi: 'He means that wiping with washing is not legislated, for wiping is an unintelligible purification, so it is confined to what the law specifies, so the intended impossibility is legal impossibility, or it means that combining them is impossible in terms of ruling.' He said: 'For there is no combining' means there is no combining in law between washing and wiping: i.e., in one duty. But when duties are multiple, combining exists, like washing the face and hands and feet with wiping the head in wudu. So if one wiped on one khuff and washed one foot, that would necessitate combining them in one duty pertaining to the feet. He said: 'The entire foot washed' – Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: 'One who wipes on his khuffs, if water enters both his khuffs and he washes his feet, his wudu is nullified...'
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the effect of removing one khuff (leather sock) on the validity of the wipe. The Hanafi ruling is that removing one sock nullifies the wipe on both feet. The author explains why the text says 'removing the khuff' in singular: to indicate that even removing one is a nullifier. The reason is that if one removes one sock, that foot must be washed in wudu (since the wipe is no longer valid on that foot). Then it becomes obligatory to wash the other foot as well, because it is impossible in Islamic law to combine washing one foot and wiping the other in the same wudu. The same applies if water enters one sock and washes the foot. The passage then cites a hadith from Ibn 'Umar where he removed both socks and washed his feet without renewing wudu, showing that the wipe only temporarily removes the state of hadath; once the sock is removed, the hadath returns to the foot. The commentary further explains the legal reasoning: the combination of washing and wiping in one duty is not legislated (wiping is a purely legal purification, not analogizable to washing). Therefore, removing one sock necessitates washing both feet, which nullifies the previous wipe entirely. If the period for wiping still remains, one may renew the wipe after performing wudu.

Key Terms
الخف (al-khuff): A leather sock that covers the foot and ankle, worn for warmth or protection, upon which wiping is permissible under certain conditions.
نزع الخف (naz' al-khuff): Removing the leather sock. In this context, it is a legal nullifier of the wipe even if only one sock is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person wearing khuffs removes only one sock while still within the wipe period?
A) The wipe remains valid on the other foot.
B) The entire wipe is nullified, and both feet must be washed in the next wudu.
C) He may wipe on the bare foot and continue wiping on the other sock.
D) He must immediately perform a new wudu without washing either foot.

Key Takeaway
Removing one khuff nullifies the wipe on both feet because the law does not permit washing one foot and wiping the other in the same wudu.
